<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Life on Mars</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars_(disambiguation)" title="Life on Mars (disambiguation)">Life on Mars (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Exobiology on Mars" redirects here. For the space mission, see <a href="/wiki/ExoMars" title="ExoMars">ExoMars</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p>The possibility of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> is a subject of significant interest to <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> due to its proximity and similarities to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. To date, no proof has been found of past or present life on Mars. Cumulative evidence shows that during the ancient <a href="/wiki/Noachian" title="Noachian">Noachian</a> time period, the surface environment of Mars had liquid water and may have been <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a> for microorganisms. The existence of habitable conditions does not necessarily indicate the presence of life.
</p><p>Scientific searches for evidence of life began in the 19th century, and they continue today via telescopic investigations and deployed probes. While early work focused on phenomenology and bordered on fantasy, the modern <a href="/wiki/Models_of_scientific_inquiry" title="Models of scientific inquiry">scientific inquiry</a> has emphasized the search for <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water</a>, chemical <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> in the soil and rocks at the planet's surface, and <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarker</a> gases in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160912_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160912-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mars is of particular interest for the study of the origins of life because of its similarity to the early Earth. This is especially so since Mars has a cold climate and lacks <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>, so it has remained almost unchanged since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Hesperian" title="Hesperian">Hesperian</a> period. At least two-thirds of Mars's surface is more than 3.5 billion years old, and Mars may thus hold the best record of the prebiotic conditions leading to <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a>, even if life does not or has never existed there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fromproto_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fromproto-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the confirmation of the past existence of surface liquid water, the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)"><i>Opportunity</i></a> rovers started searching for evidence of past life, including a past <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemotroph" title="Chemotroph">chemotrophic</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemolithotrophs" title="Chemolithotrophs">chemolithoautotrophic</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, as well as ancient water, including <a href="/wiki/Lacustrine_plain" title="Lacustrine plain">fluvio-lacustrine environments</a> (<a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a> related to ancient rivers or lakes) that may have been <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140124a_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140124a-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140124special_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140124special-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140124_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140124-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140124c_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140124c-8">[8]</a></sup> The search for evidence of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomy</a> (related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a> on Mars is now a primary NASA and ESA objective.
</p><p>The findings of organic compounds inside sedimentary rocks and of <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> on Mars are of interest as they are precursors for prebiotic chemistry. Such findings, along with previous discoveries that liquid water was clearly present on ancient Mars, further supports the possible early habitability of <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale Crater</a> on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GPL-20170905_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-GPL-20170905-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GZ-20170906_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-GZ-20170906-10">[10]</a></sup> Currently, the surface of Mars is bathed with radiation, and when reacting with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perchlorates" title="Perchlorates">perchlorates</a> on the surface, it may be more toxic to <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> than thought earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SM-20170706_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-SM-20170706-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170706_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170706-12">[12]</a></sup>  Therefore, the consensus is that if life exists —or existed— on Mars, it could be found or is best preserved in the subsurface, away from present-day harsh surface processes.
</p><p>In June 2018 NASA announced the detection of seasonal variation of methane levels on Mars. Methane could be produced by microorganisms or by geological means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607-13">[13]</a></sup> The European <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a> started mapping the atmospheric methane in April 2018, and the 2020 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExoMars_rover" title="ExoMars rover">ExoMars rover</a> will drill and analyze subsurface samples, while the NASA <a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> rover will cache dozens of drill samples for their potential transport to Earth laboratories in the late 2020s or 2030s.  
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_speculation">Early speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early speculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:448px;max-width:448px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="713" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/220px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/330px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/440px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Historical map of Mars from <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Schiaparelli" title="Giovanni Schiaparelli">Giovanni Schiaparelli</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="800" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/220px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/330px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/440px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Mars canals illustrated by astronomer <a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Percival Lowell</a>, 1898</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps" title="Martian polar ice caps">Mars' polar ice caps</a> were discovered in the mid-17th century. In the late 18th century, <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> proved they grow and shrink alternately, in the summer and winter of each hemisphere. By the mid-19th century, astronomers knew that <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> had certain other similarities to Earth, for example that the <a href="/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars" title="Timekeeping on Mars">length of a day on Mars</a> was almost the same as a day on Earth. They also knew that its <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> was similar to Earth's, which meant it experienced seasons just as Earth does — but of nearly double the length owing to its <a href="/wiki/Darian_calendar" title="Darian calendar">much longer year</a>. These observations led to increase in speculation that the darker <a href="/wiki/Albedo_feature" title="Albedo feature">albedo features</a> were water and the brighter ones were land, whence followed speculation on whether Mars may be inhabited by some form of life.
</p><p>In 1854, <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a>, a fellow of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a>, Cambridge, who popularized the word <i>scientist,</i> theorized that Mars had seas, land and possibly life forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Speculation about life on Mars exploded in the late 19th century, following telescopic observation by some observers of apparent <a href="/wiki/Martian_canal" title="Martian canal">Martian canals</a> — which were later found to be optical illusions. Despite this, in 1895, American astronomer <a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Percival Lowell</a> published his book <i>Mars,</i> followed by <i>Mars and its Canals</i> in 1906,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151001_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151001-15">[15]</a></sup> proposing that the canals were the work of a long-gone civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This idea led British writer <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> to write <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds_(novel)" title="The War of the Worlds (novel)">The War of the Worlds</a></i> in 1897, telling of an invasion by aliens from Mars who were fleeing the planet's desiccation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">Spectroscopic</a> analysis of Mars' atmosphere began in earnest in 1894, when U.S. astronomer <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace_Campbell" title="William Wallace Campbell">William Wallace Campbell</a> showed that neither water nor oxygen were present in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_atmosphere" title="Martian atmosphere">Martian atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chambers_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-17">[17]</a></sup> By 1909, better telescopes and the best perihelic opposition of Mars since 1877 conclusively put an end to the canal hypothesis.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability">Habitability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Habitability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars#Conditions_for_human_habitation" title="Colonization of Mars">Colonization of Mars § Conditions for human habitation</a></div>
<p>Chemical, physical, geological, and geographic attributes shape the environments on Mars. Isolated measurements of these factors may be insufficient to deem an environment habitable, but the sum of measurements can help predict locations with greater or lesser habitability potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_LPS_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-2013_LPS-18">[18]</a></sup>  The two current ecological approaches for predicting the potential habitability of the Martian surface use 19 or 20 environmental factors, with an emphasis on water availability, temperature, the presence of nutrients, an energy source, and protection from solar ultraviolet and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">galactic cosmic radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D.C.Golden_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-D.C.Golden-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaty_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaty-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists do not know the minimum number of parameters for determination of habitability potential, but they are certain it is greater than one or two of the factors in the table below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_LPS_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-2013_LPS-18">[18]</a></sup> Similarly, for each group of parameters, the habitability threshold for each is to be determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_LPS_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-2013_LPS-18">[18]</a></sup> Laboratory simulations show that whenever multiple lethal factors are combined, the survival rates plummet quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust-up_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-dust-up-21">[21]</a></sup> There are no full-Mars simulations published yet that include all of the biocidal factors combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust-up_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-dust-up-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Past">Past</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Past">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recent models have shown that, even with a dense <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> atmosphere, early Mars was colder than Earth has ever been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitability_2013_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-habitability_2013-22">[22]</a></sup> Transiently warm conditions related to impacts or volcanism could have produced conditions favoring the formation of the late <a href="/wiki/Noachian" title="Noachian">Noachian</a> valley networks, even though the mid-late Noachian global conditions were probably icy. Local warming of the environment by volcanism and impacts would have been sporadic, but there should have been many events of water flowing at the surface of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitability_2013_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-habitability_2013-22">[22]</a></sup> Both the mineralogical and the morphological evidence indicates a degradation of habitability from the mid <a href="/wiki/Hesperian" title="Hesperian">Hesperian</a> onward. The exact causes are not well understood but may be related to a combination of processes including loss of early atmosphere, or impact erosion, or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitability_2013_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-habitability_2013-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="938" data-file-width="1551" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/220px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/330px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/440px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_craters_on_Mars:_A-G" title="List of craters on Mars: A-G">Alga crater</a> is thought to have deposits of  <a href="/wiki/Impactite" title="Impactite">impact glass</a> that may have preserved ancient <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a>, if present during the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150608_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150608-23">[23]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The loss of the Martian <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetic field</a> strongly affected surface environments through atmospheric loss and increased radiation; this change significantly degraded surface habitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biosignatures_2011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biosignatures_2011-24">[24]</a></sup> When there was a magnetic field, the atmosphere would have been protected from erosion by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, which would ensure the maintenance of a dense atmosphere, necessary for liquid water to exist on the surface of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehant_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dehant-25">[25]</a></sup> The loss of the atmosphere was accompanied by decreasing temperatures. Part of the liquid water inventory sublimed and was transported to the poles, while the rest became
trapped in <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, a subsurface ice layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitability_2013_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-habitability_2013-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Observations on Earth and numerical modeling have shown that a crater-forming impact can result in the creation of a long-lasting <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hydrothermal system</a> when ice is present in the crust. For example, a 130 km large crater could sustain an active hydrothermal system for up to 2 million years, that is, long enough for microscopic life to emerge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitability_2013_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-habitability_2013-22">[22]</a></sup> but unlikely to have progressed any further down the evolutionary path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soil and rock samples studied in 2013 by NASA's <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover's</a> onboard instruments brought about additional information on several habitability factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ancient_life_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ancient_life-27">[27]</a></sup> The rover team identified some of the key chemical ingredients for life in this soil, including <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, as well as clay minerals, suggesting a long-ago aqueous environment — perhaps a lake or an ancient streambed — that had neutral acidity and not too salty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ancient_life_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-ancient_life-27">[27]</a></sup> On December 9, 2013, NASA reported that, based on evidence from <i>Curiosity</i> studying <a href="/wiki/Aeolis_Palus" title="Aeolis Palus">Aeolis Palus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale Crater</a> contained an ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_lake" title="Freshwater lake">freshwater lake</a> which could have been a hospitable environment for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbial_life" title="Microbial life">microbial life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131209_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131209-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20131209_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20131209-29">[29]</a></sup>  The confirmation that liquid water once flowed on Mars, the existence of nutrients, and the previous discovery of a past <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetic field</a> that protected the planet from cosmic and solar radiation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> together strongly suggest that Mars could have had the environmental factors to support life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The assessment of past habitability is not in itself evidence that Martian life has ever actually existed. If it did, it was probably <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial</a>, existing communally in fluids or on sediments, either free-living or as <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biosignatures_2011_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biosignatures_2011-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Impactite" title="Impactite">Impactite</a>, shown to preserve signs of life on Earth, was discovered on Mars and could contain signs of ancient life, if life ever existed on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 7, 2018, NASA announced that the <i>Curiosity</i> rover had discovered organic molecules in sedimentary rocks dating to three billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608c_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608c-36">[36]</a></sup> The detection of organic molecules in rocks indicate that some of the building blocks for life were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180607_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180607-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present">Present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conceivably, if life exists (or existed) on Mars, evidence of that life could be found, or is best preserved, in the subsurface, away from present-day harsh surface conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-39">[39]</a></sup> Present-day life on Mars, or its biosignatures, could occur kilometers below the surface, or in subsurface geothermal hot spots, or it could occur a few meters below the surface. The <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> layer on Mars is only a couple of centimeters below the surface, and salty <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brines</a> can be liquid a few centimeters below that but not far down. Water is close to its boiling point even at the deepest points in the Hellas basin, and so cannot remain liquid for long on the surface of Mars in its present state, except when covered in ice or after a sudden release of underground water.
</p><p>So far, NASA has pursued a "follow the water" strategy on Mars and has not searched for biosignatures for life there directly since the <i>Viking</i> mission. As of 2017, the consensus by astrobiologists at NASA is that it may be necessary to access the Martian subsurface to find currently habitable environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dormant_subsurface_life">Dormant subsurface life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dormant subsurface life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Curiosity measured ionizing radiation levels of 76 mGy a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This level of ionizing radiation is sterilizing for dormant life on the surface of Mars. It varies considerably in  habitability depending on its orbital eccentricity and the tilt of its axis. If the surface life has been reanimated as recently as 450,000 years ago, then rovers on Mars could find dormant but still viable life at a depth of one meter below the surface, according to an estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_radiation">Cosmic radiation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cosmic radiation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1965, the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_4" title="Mariner 4">Mariner 4</a> probe discovered that Mars had no <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">global magnetic field</a> that would protect the planet from potentially life-threatening <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>; observations made in the late 1990s by the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a> confirmed this discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Scientists speculate that the lack of magnetic shielding helped the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> blow away much of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Mars's atmosphere</a> over the course of several billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> As a result, the planet has been vulnerable to radiation from space for about 4 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hostile_to_life_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-hostile_to_life-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent <i>in-situ</i> data from <i>Curiosity</i> rover indicates that <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">galactic cosmic rays</a> (GCR) and <a href="/wiki/Solar_particle_event" title="Solar particle event">solar particle events</a> (SPE) may not be a limiting factor in habitability assessments for present-day surface life on Mars. The level of 76 mGy per year measured by <i>Curiosity</i> is similar to levels inside the ISS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In the 2014 Findings of the Second MEPAG Special Regions Science Analysis Group, their conclusion was:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RummelBeaty2014_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-RummelBeaty2014-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"From MSL RAD measurements, ionizing radiation from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">galactic cosmic rays</a> (GCR) at Mars is so low as to be negligible. Intermittent <a href="/wiki/Solar_particle_event" title="Solar particle event">Solar particle events</a> (SPE) can increase the atmospheric ionization down to ground level and increase the total dose, but these events are sporadic and last at most a few (2–5) days. These facts are not used to distinguish Special Regions on Mars." A Special Region is defined as a region on the Mars surface where Earth life could potentially survive.</dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulative_effects">Cumulative effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cumulative effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Even the hardiest cells known could not possibly survive the cosmic radiation near the surface of Mars since Mars lost its protective magnetosphere and atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_radiation_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_radiation-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> After mapping cosmic radiation levels at various depths on Mars, researchers have concluded that over time, any life within the first several meters of the planet's surface would be killed by lethal doses of cosmic radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_radiation_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_radiation-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartnell_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartnell-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartnell_Geographic_with_quote_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartnell_Geographic_with_quote-50">[50]</a></sup> The team calculated that the cumulative damage to <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> by cosmic radiation would limit retrieving viable dormant cells on Mars to depths greater than 7.5 meters below the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartnell_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dartnell-49">[49]</a></sup>
Even the most radiation-tolerant Earthly bacteria would survive in dormant <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spore</a> state only 18,000 years at the surface; at 2 meters  —the greatest depth at which the <a href="/wiki/ExoMars" title="ExoMars">ExoMars</a> rover will be capable of reaching— survival time would be 90,000 to half a million years, depending on the type of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartnell_Geographic_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartnell_Geographic-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Data collected by the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_assessment_detector" title="Radiation assessment detector">Radiation assessment detector</a> (RAD) instrument on board the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> revealed that the absorbed dose measured is 76 <a href="/wiki/Gray_(unit)" title="Gray (unit)">mGy</a>/year at the surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD_January_2014_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAD_January_2014-52">[52]</a></sup> and that "<a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> strongly influences chemical compositions and structures, especially for water, salts, and redox-sensitive components such as organic molecules."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD_January_2014_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-RAD_January_2014-52">[52]</a></sup> Regardless of the source of Martian <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> (meteoric, geological, or biological), its carbon bonds are susceptible to breaking and reconfiguring with surrounding elements by ionizing charged particle radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD_January_2014_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-RAD_January_2014-52">[52]</a></sup>  These improved subsurface radiation estimates give insight into the potential for the preservation of possible organic <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> as a function of depth as well as survival times of possible microbial or bacterial life forms left dormant beneath the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD_January_2014_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-RAD_January_2014-52">[52]</a></sup> The report concludes that the <i>in situ</i> "surface measurements —and subsurface estimates— constrain the preservation window for Martian organic matter following exhumation and exposure to ionizing radiation in the top few meters of the Martian surface."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD_January_2014_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-RAD_January_2014-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2017, NASA reported <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation levels</a> on the surface of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> were temporarily doubled and were associated with an <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a> 25 times brighter than any observed earlier, due to a major, and unexpected, <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">solar storm</a> in the middle of the month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20170930_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20170930-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UV_radiation">UV radiation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: UV radiation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On UV radiation, a 2014 report concludes <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RummelBeaty2014_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-RummelBeaty2014-46">[46]</a></sup> that "[T]he Martian UV radiation environment is rapidly lethal to unshielded microbes but can be attenuated by global dust storms and shielded completely by &lt; 1 mm of regolith or by other organisms."  In addition, laboratory research published in July 2017 demonstrated that UV irradiated perchlorates cause a 10.8-fold increase in cell death when compared to cells exposed to UV radiation after 60 seconds of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacteriocidal_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-bacteriocidal-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxides_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxides-55">[55]</a></sup> The penetration depth of UV radiation into soils is in the sub-millimeter to millimeter range and depends on the properties of the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxides_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxides-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Perchlorates">Perchlorates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Perchlorates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Martian regolith is known to contain a maximum of 0.5% (w/v) <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorate</a> (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) that is toxic for most living organisms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> but since they drastically lower the freezing point of water and few extremophiles can use it as an energy source (see <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate#Biology" title="Perchlorate">Perchlorates - Biology</a>), it has prompted speculation of what their influence would be on habitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacteriocidal_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-bacteriocidal-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research published in July 2017 shows that when irradiated with a simulated Martian UV flux, perchlorates become even more lethal to bacteria (<a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bactericide</a>). Even dormant spores lost viability within minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacteriocidal_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-bacteriocidal-54">[54]</a></sup> In addition, two other compounds of the Martian surface, <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, act in synergy with irradiated perchlorates to cause a 10.8-fold increase in cell death when compared to cells exposed to UV radiation after 60 seconds of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacteriocidal_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-bacteriocidal-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxides_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-oxides-55">[55]</a></sup>  It was also found that abraded silicates (quartz and basalt) lead to the formation of toxic <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The researchers concluded that "the surface of Mars is lethal to vegetative cells and renders much of the surface and near-surface regions uninhabitable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> This research demonstrates that the present-day surface is more uninhabitable than previously thought,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacteriocidal_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-bacteriocidal-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall2017_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall2017-61">[61]</a></sup> and reinforces the notion to inspect at least a few meters into the ground to ensure the levels of radiation would be relatively low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall2017_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wall2017-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recurrent_slope_lineae">Recurrent slope lineae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recurrent slope lineae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seasonal_flows_on_warm_Martian_slopes" title="Seasonal flows on warm Martian slopes">Recurrent slope lineae</a> (RSL) features form on Sun-facing slopes at times of the year when the local temperatures reach above the melting point for ice. The streaks grow in spring, widen in late summer and then fade away in autumn. This is hard to model in any other way except as involving liquid water in some form, though the streaks themselves are thought to be a secondary effect and not a direct indication of the dampness of the regolith. Although these features are now confirmed to involve liquid water in some form, the water could be either too cold or too salty for life. At present they are treated as potentially habitable, as "Uncertain Regions, to be treated as Special Regions".
</p><p>The 'Special Regions assessment' says of them:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RummelBeaty2014_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-RummelBeaty2014-46">[46]</a></sup> "Although no single model currently proposed for the origin of RSL adequately explains all observations, they are currently best interpreted as being due to the seepage of water at &gt; 250 K, with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a_{w}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>w</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{w}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a_w" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a12aa5db11b8e78a6f0fa27d105e3f5c7b8d0d7" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.639ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> (<a href="/wiki/Water_activity" title="Water activity">water activity</a>) unknown and perhaps variable. As such they meet the criteria for Uncertain Regions, to be treated as Special Regions. There are other features on Mars with characteristics similar to RSL, but their relationship to possible liquid water is much less likely." They were first reported in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> They were already suspected as involving flowing brines back then, as all the other models available involved liquid water in some form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150928b_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150928b-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150928a_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150928a-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ojhaetal2015_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ojhaetal2015-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The thermodynamic availability of water (<a href="/wiki/Water_activity" title="Water activity">water activity</a>) strictly limits microbial propagation on Earth, particularly in hypersaline environments, and there are indications that the brine ionic strength is a barrier to the habitability of Mars. Experiments show that high <a href="/wiki/Ionic_strength" title="Ionic strength">ionic strength</a>, driven to extremes on Mars by the ubiquitous occurrence of divalent ions, "renders these environments uninhabitable despite the presence of biologically available water."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen_fixation">Nitrogen fixation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Nitrogen fixation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After carbon, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> is arguably the most important element needed for life. Thus, measurements of <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> over the range of 0.1% to 5% are required to address the question of its occurrence and distribution. There is nitrogen (as N<sub>2</sub>) in the atmosphere at low levels, but this is not adequate to support <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a> for biological incorporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Icebreaker2018_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Icebreaker2018-69">[69]</a></sup> Nitrogen in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> could be a resource for human exploration both as a nutrient for plant growth and for use in chemical processes. On Earth, nitrates correlate with perchlorates in desert environments, and this may also be true on Mars. Nitrate is expected to be stable on Mars and to have formed by thermal shock from impact or volcanic plume lightning on ancient Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2015_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2015-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 March 2015, NASA reported that the <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">SAM</a> instrument on the <i>Curiosity</i> rover detected nitrates by heating surface sediments. The nitrogen in nitrate is in a "fixed" state, meaning that it is in an oxidized form that can be used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_organisms" title="Living organisms">living organisms</a>. The discovery supports the notion that ancient Mars may have been hospitable for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2015_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern_2015-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150324_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150324-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> It is suspected that all nitrate on Mars is a relic, with no modern contribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nitrogen_2017_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nitrogen_2017-73">[73]</a></sup> Nitrate abundance ranges from non-detection to 681 ± 304 mg/kg in the samples examined until late 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nitrogen_2017_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nitrogen_2017-73">[73]</a></sup>  Modeling indicates that the transient condensed water films on the surface should be transported to lower depths (≈10 m) potentially transporting nitrates, where subsurface microorganisms could thrive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast, phosphate, one of the chemical nutrients thought to be essential for life, is readily available on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_pressure">Low pressure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Low pressure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Further complicating estimates of the habitability of the Martian surface is the fact that very little is known about the growth of microorganisms at pressures close to those on the surface of Mars. Some teams determined that some bacteria may be capable of cellular replication down to 25 mbar, but that is still above the atmospheric pressures found on Mars (range 1–14 mbar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serratia_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serratia-76">[76]</a></sup> In another study, twenty-six strains of bacteria were chosen based on their recovery from spacecraft assembly facilities, and only <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Serratia_liquefaciens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Serratia liquefaciens (page does not exist)">Serratia liquefaciens</a></i> strain ATCC 27592 exhibited growth at 7 mbar, 0 °C, and CO<sub>2</sub>-enriched anoxic atmospheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serratia_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Serratia-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_water">Liquid water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Liquid water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">Water on Mars</a></div>
<p>Liquid water is a necessary but not sufficient condition for life as we know it, as habitability is a function of a multitude of environmental parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151015_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151015-77">[77]</a></sup> Liquid water cannot exist on the surface of Mars except at the lowest elevations for minutes or hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>  Liquid water does not appear at the surface itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> but it could form in minuscule amounts around dust particles in snow heated by the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shiga2009_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shiga2009-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.softpedia_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.softpedia-82">[82]</a></sup> Also, the ancient equatorial ice sheets beneath the ground may slowly sublimate or melt, accessible from the surface via caves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>

Planitia</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Scalloped_topography" title="Scalloped topography">Scalloped terrain</a> led to the discovery of a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/PIA21138_-_Location_of_Large_Subsurface_Water-Ice_Deposit_in_Utopia_Planitia%2C_Mars.png 1.5x" width="443"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>Map of terrain</center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>Water on Mars exists almost exclusively as water ice, located in the <a href="/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps" title="Martian polar ice caps">Martian polar ice caps</a> and under the shallow Martian surface even at more temperate latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars.jpl.nasa.gov_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-mars.jpl.nasa.gov-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feildman,_T._2004_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feildman,_T._2004-91">[91]</a></sup> A small amount of water vapor is present in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucar_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucar-92">[92]</a></sup>  There are no bodies of liquid water on the Martian surface because its atmospheric pressure at the surface averages 600 pascals (0.087 psi)—about 0.6% of Earth's mean sea level pressure—and because the temperature is far too low, (210 K (−63 °C)) leading to immediate freezing. Despite this, about 3.8 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-93">[93]</a></sup> there was a denser <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere</a>, higher temperature, and vast amounts of liquid water flowed on the surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flashback_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-flashback-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clay_clues_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clay_clues-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> including large oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2003JGRE..108.5042C_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-2003JGRE..108.5042C-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFN-20130125_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFN-20130125-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2554" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg/220px-History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg/330px-History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg/440px-History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:History_of_Water_on_Mars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A series of artist's conceptions of past water coverage on Mars</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1448" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/150px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/225px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/300px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Mars <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">SouthPole</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">Site of Subglacial Water</a><br/>(25 July 2018)</center></div></div></div>
<p>It has been estimated that the primordial oceans on Mars would have covered between 36%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-103">[103]</a></sup>  and 75% of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-104">[104]</a></sup> On November 22, 2016, NASA reported finding a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">underground ice</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Utopia_Planitia" title="Utopia Planitia">Utopia Planitia</a> region of Mars. The volume of water detected has been estimated to be equivalent to the volume of water in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20161122_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20161122-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Register-2016_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Register-2016-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20161122jpl_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20161122jpl-89">[89]</a></sup>
Analysis of Martian sandstones, using data obtained from orbital spectrometry, suggests that the waters that previously existed on the surface of Mars would have had too high a salinity to support most Earth-like life. Tosca <i>et al.</i> found that the Martian water in the locations they studied all had <a href="/wiki/Water_activity" title="Water activity">water activity</a>, a<sub>w</sub> ≤ 0.78 to 0.86—a level fatal to most Terrestrial life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haloarchaea" title="Haloarchaea">Haloarchaea</a>, however, are able to live in hypersaline solutions, up to the saturation point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2000, possible evidence for current liquid water flowing at the surface of Mars was discovered in the form of flood-like gullies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underground_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-underground-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>  Additional similar images were published in 2006, taken by the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a>, that suggested that water occasionally flows on the surface of Mars. The images showed changes in steep crater walls and sediment deposits, providing the strongest evidence yet that water coursed through them as recently as several years ago.
</p><p>There is  disagreement in the scientific community as to whether or not the recent gully streaks were formed by liquid water. Some suggest the flows were merely dry sand flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moon_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-moon-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Others suggest it may be liquid <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> near the surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voanews_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-voanews-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ames_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ames-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> but the exact source of the water and the mechanism behind its motion are not understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hirise_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-hirise-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, scientists reported the discovery of a subglacial lake on Mars, 1.5 km (0.93 mi) below the <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">southern polar ice cap</a>, and extending sideways about 20 km (12 mi), the first known stable body of water on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180725_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180725-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180725_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180725-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The lake was discovered using the <a href="/wiki/MARSIS" title="MARSIS">MARSIS</a> radar on board the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a></i> orbiter, and the profiles were collected between May 2012 and December 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suppl_material_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suppl_material-121">[121]</a></sup> The lake is centered at 193°E, 81°S, a flat area that does not exhibit any peculiar topographic characteristics but is surrounded by higher ground, except on its eastern side, where there is a depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180725_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180725-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silica">Silica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Silica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/220px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/330px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/440px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The silica-rich patch discovered by <a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)">Spirit rover</a></div></div></div>
<p>In May 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)"><i>Spirit</i> rover</a> disturbed a patch of ground with its inoperative wheel, uncovering an area 90% rich in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The feature is reminiscent of the effect of <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot spring</a> water or steam coming into contact with volcanic rocks. Scientists consider this as evidence of a past environment that may have been favorable for microbial life and theorize that one possible origin for the silica may have been produced by the interaction of soil with acid vapors produced by volcanic activity in the presence of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20071210a_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-20071210a-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on Earth analogs, <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal systems</a> on Mars would be highly attractive for their potential for preserving <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic</a> <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leveille_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leveille-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>  For this reason, hydrothermal deposits are regarded as important targets in the exploration for fossil evidence of ancient Martian life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_biosignatures">Possible biosignatures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Possible biosignatures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In May 2017, evidence of the <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">earliest known life</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_land" title="Colonization of land">on land</a> on Earth may have been found in 3.48-billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Geyserite" title="Geyserite">geyserite</a> and other related mineral deposits (often found around <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>) uncovered in the <a href="/wiki/Pilbara_Craton" title="Pilbara Craton">Pilbara Craton</a> of Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20170509_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20170509-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20170509_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20170509-131">[131]</a></sup> These findings may be helpful in deciding where best to search for <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">early signs of life</a> on the planet Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20170509_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20170509-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20170509_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20170509-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane">Methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Atmosphere of Mars § Methane</a></div>
<p>Possible trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in the atmosphere of Mars were first discovered in 2003 with Earth-based telescopes and fully verified in 2004 by the ESA Mars Express spacecraft in orbit around Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> As methane is an unstable gas, its presence indicates that there must be an active source on the planet in order to keep such levels in the atmosphere. It is estimated that Mars must produce 270 ton/year of methane,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> impacts account for only 0.8% of the total methane production. Although geologic sources of methane such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpentinization" title="Serpentinization">serpentinization</a> are possible, the lack of current <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_activity" title="Hydrothermal activity">hydrothermal activity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspots</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thermal_signature_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-thermal_signature-140">[140]</a></sup> are not favorable for geologic methane. It has been suggested that the methane was produced by chemical reactions in meteorites, driven by the intense heat during entry through the atmosphere. Although research published in December 2009 ruled out this possibility,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> research published in 2012 suggest that a source may be <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> on meteorites that are converted to methane by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg/300px-PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg/450px-PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg/600px-PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22328-MarsCuriosityRover-Methane-SeasonalCycle-20180607.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i>Curiosity</i> detected a cyclical seasonal variation in atmospheric methane (June 7, 2018).</center></div></div></div>
<p>The existence of life in the form of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> is among possible but as yet unproven sources. Methanogens do not require oxygen or organic nutrients, are non-photosynthetic, use hydrogen as their energy source and carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) as their carbon source, so they could exist in subsurface environments on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mickol_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mickol-143">[143]</a></sup> If microscopic Martian life is producing the methane, it probably resides far below the surface, where it is still warm enough for liquid water to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steigerwald_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steigerwald-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 2003 discovery of methane in the atmosphere, some scientists have been designing models and <i>in vitro</i> experiments testing the growth of <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> bacteria on simulated Martian soil, where all four methanogen strains tested produced substantial levels of methane, even in the presence of 1.0wt% <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorate</a> salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kral_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kral-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>A team led by Levin suggested that both phenomena—methane production and degradation—could be accounted for by an ecology of methane-producing and methane-consuming microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howe_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howe-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_2009_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin_2009-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martian_Methane_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Martian_Methane_Map.jpg/220px-Martian_Methane_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Martian_Methane_Map.jpg/330px-Martian_Methane_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Martian_Methane_Map.jpg/440px-Martian_Methane_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martian_Methane_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Distribution of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in the atmosphere of Mars in the Northern Hemisphere during summer</center></div></div></div>
<p>Research at the University of Arkansas presented in June 2015 suggested that some methanogens could survive on Mars's low pressure.  Rebecca Mickol found that in her laboratory, four species of methanogens survived low-pressure conditions that were similar to a subsurface liquid aquifer on Mars.  The four species that she tested were <i><a href="/wiki/Methanothermobacter" title="Methanothermobacter">Methanothermobacter</a> wolfeii</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Methanosarcina" title="Methanosarcina">Methanosarcina</a> barkeri</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Methanobacterium" title="Methanobacterium">Methanobacterium</a> formicicum</i>, and  <i><a href="/wiki/Methanococcus_maripaludis" title="Methanococcus maripaludis">Methanococcus maripaludis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mickol_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mickol-143">[143]</a></sup> In June 2012, scientists reported that measuring the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> levels on Mars may help determine the likelihood of life on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20120607_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20120607-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120625_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120625-149">[149]</a></sup> According to the scientists, "...low H<sub>2</sub>/CH<sub>4</sub> ratios (less than approximately 40) indicate that life is likely present and active."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20120607_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20120607-148">[148]</a></sup> Other scientists have recently reported methods of detecting hydrogen and methane in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmospheres" title="Extraterrestrial atmospheres">extraterrestrial atmospheres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120627_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120627-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired-20120627_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-20120627-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>, which landed on Mars in August 2012, is able to make measurements that distinguish between different isotopologues of methane, and in 2014, Curiosity <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mars_Science_Laboratory#Detection_of_organics" title="Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory">detected</a> a "tenfold spike" in the level of methane in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmosphere</a> compared to the usual background readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Even if the mission is to determine that microscopic Martian life is the seasonal source of the methane, the life forms probably reside far below the surface, outside of the rover's reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The first measurements with the <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">Tunable Laser Spectrometer (TLS)</a> in the <i>Curiosity</i> rover indicated that there is less than 5 ppb of methane at the landing site at the point of the measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science-20121102_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science-20121102-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20121102_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20121102-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20121102_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20121102-159">[159]</a></sup>  On July 19, 2013, NASA scientists published the results of a new analysis of the atmosphere of Mars, reporting a lack of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gale_Crater" title="Gale Crater">landing site</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired-20130718_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-20130718-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130719a_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130719a-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130719b_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130719b-162">[162]</a></sup> On September 19, 2013, NASA again reported no detection of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a> with a measured value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999180000000000000♠</span>0.18<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.67</span> ppbv corresponding to an upper limit of only 1.3 ppbv (95% confidence limit) and, as a result, concluded that the probability of current methanogenic microbial activity on Mars is reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJ-20130919_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-SJ-20130919-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130919_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130919-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130919_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130919-165">[165]</a></sup> On 16 December 2014, NASA reported that Curiosity had detected a tenfold increase ('spike') in methane in the atmosphere around it in late 2013 and early 2014. Four measurements taken over two months in this period averaged 7 ppb, suggesting that methane is released at intervals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 7, 2018, NASA announced it has detected a seasonal variation of methane levels on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607-13">[13]</a></sup> Chris Webster, a senior researcher at Jet Propulsion Laboratory says, "Not only have we got this wonderful repeatability, but the seasonal cycle changes by a factor of three. That's a huge change, completely unexpected. And what it does, it gives us a key to unlocking the mysteries associated with Mars methane because now we have something to test our models and our understanding against." Methane lasts only a few centuries before it is broken down. He also says "...so if we see methane in the martian atmosphere, that means something is happening today, it's being released or it's being created."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607vid_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607vid-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180607_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180607-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voosen_Science_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voosen_Science-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608a_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608a-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608b_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608b-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608c_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608c-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a>, launched in March 2016, began on 21 April 2018 to map the concentration and sources of methane in the atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space20180223_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-space20180223-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> as well as its decomposition products such as <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formaldehyde">Formaldehyde</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Formaldehyde">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In February 2005, it was announced that the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Fourier_Spectrometer" title="Planetary Fourier Spectrometer">Planetary Fourier Spectrometer</a> (PFS) on the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Express_Orbiter" title="Mars Express Orbiter">Mars Express Orbiter</a> had detected traces of <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a>.
