Vittorio Formisano, the director of the PFS, has speculated that the formaldehyde could be the byproduct of the oxidation of methane and, according to him, would provide evidence that Mars is either extremely geologically active or harboring colonies of microbial life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> NASA scientists consider the preliminary findings well worth a follow-up but have also rejected the claims of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PFS_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-PFS-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Releasease_:_05-052_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Releasease_:_05-052-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viking_lander_biological_experiments">Viking lander biological experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Viking lander biological experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_spacecraft_biological_experiments" title="Viking spacecraft biological experiments">Viking spacecraft biological experiments</a></div>
<p>The 1970s <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking program</a> placed two identical landers on the surface of Mars tasked to look for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biosignatures" title="Biosignatures">biosignatures</a> of microbial life on the surface. Of the four experiments performed by each Viking lander, only the 'Labeled Release' (LR) experiment gave a positive result for <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, while the other three did not detect <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. The LR was a specific experiment designed to test only a narrowly defined critical aspect of the theory concerning the possibility of life on Mars; therefore, the overall results were declared inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chambers_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-17">[17]</a></sup> No Mars lander mission has found meaningful traces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomolecules" title="Biomolecules">biomolecules</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biosignatures" title="Biosignatures">biosignatures</a>.  The claim of extant microbial life on Mars is based on old data collected by the Viking landers, currently reinterpreted as sufficient evidence of life, mainly by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Levin" title="Gilbert Levin">Gilbert Levin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-177">[177]</a></sup> Joseph D. Miller,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Navarro,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Contreras2008_1_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Contreras2008_1-179">[179]</a></sup> Giorgio Bianciardi and Patricia Ann Straat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bianciardi-2012_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bianciardi-2012-180">[180]</a></sup> that the Viking LR experiments detected extant microbial life on Mars.
</p><p>Assessments published in December 2010 by Rafael Navarro–Gonzáles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reanalysis_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-reanalysis-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro-184">[184]</a></sup> indicate that organic compounds "could have been present" in the soil analyzed by both Viking 1 and 2. The study determined that <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorate</a> —discovered in 2008 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenix_lander" title="Phoenix lander">Phoenix lander</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>— can destroy organic compounds when heated, and produce <a href="/wiki/Chloromethane" title="Chloromethane">chloromethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dichloromethane" title="Dichloromethane">dichloromethane</a> as a byproduct, the identical chlorine compounds discovered by both Viking landers when they performed the same tests on Mars. Because perchlorate would have broken down any Martian organics, the question of whether or not Viking found organic compounds is still wide open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Labeled Release evidence was not generally accepted initially, and, to this day lacks the consensus of the scientific community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dead_or_Alive_2014_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dead_or_Alive_2014-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Curiosity_rover_sediment_sampling"><i>Curiosity</i> rover sediment sampling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Curiosity rover sediment sampling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 2018, NASA reported that the <i>Curiosity</i> rover had found evidence of complex organic compounds from <a href="/wiki/Mudstone" title="Mudstone">mudstone</a> rocks aged approximately 3.5 billion years old, sampled from two distinct sites in a dry lake in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pahrump_Hills" title="Pahrump Hills">Pahrump Hills</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale crater</a>. The rock samples, when <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolyzed</a> via the <i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">Sample Analysis at Mars</a> instrument, released an array of organic molecules; these include sulfur-containing <a href="/wiki/Thiophene" title="Thiophene">thiophenes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a> compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliphatic" title="Aliphatic">aliphatic</a> compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butene" title="Butene">butene</a>.  The concentration of organic compounds is 100-fold higher than earlier measurements. The authors speculate that the presence of sulfur may have helped preserve them. The products resemble those obtained from the breakdown of <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>, a precursor to oil and natural gas on Earth. NASA stated that these findings are not evidence that life existed on the planet, but that the organic compounds needed to sustain microscopic life were present and there may be deeper sources of organic compounds on the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voosen_Science_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voosen_Science-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608a_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608a-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608b_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608b-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608c_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608c-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorites">Meteorites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Meteorites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2018, there are 117 known <a href="/wiki/Martian_meteorite" title="Martian meteorite">Martian meteorites</a> (some of which were found in several fragments). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> These are valuable because they are the only physical samples of Mars available to Earth-bound laboratories. Some researchers have argued that microscopic <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/morphology" title="wikt:morphology">morphological</a> features found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">ALH84001</a> are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/biomorph" title="wikt:biomorph">biomorphs</a>, however this interpretation has been highly controversial and is not supported by the majority of researchers in the field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteoritos-Bio_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteoritos-Bio-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Seven criteria have been established for the recognition of past life within terrestrial geologic samples. Those criteria are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteoritos-Bio_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-meteoritos-Bio-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Is the geologic context of the sample compatible with past life?</li>
<li>Is the age of the sample and its stratigraphic location compatible with possible life?</li>
<li>Does the sample contain evidence of cellular morphology and colonies?</li>
<li>Is there any evidence of biominerals showing chemical or mineral disequilibria?</li>
<li>Is there any evidence of stable isotope patterns unique to biology?</li>
<li>Are there any organic biomarkers present?</li>
<li>Are the features indigenous to the sample?</li></ol>
<p>For general acceptance of past life in a geologic sample, essentially most or all of these criteria must be met. All seven criteria have not yet been met for any of the Martian samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteoritos-Bio_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-meteoritos-Bio-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="ALH84001">ALH84001</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: ALH84001">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001_structures.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="1987" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ALH84001_structures.jpg/220px-ALH84001_structures.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ALH84001_structures.jpg/330px-ALH84001_structures.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ALH84001_structures.jpg/440px-ALH84001_structures.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001_structures.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An electron microscope reveals bacteria-like structures in meteorite fragment <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">ALH84001</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1996, the Martian meteorite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">ALH84001</a>, a specimen that is much older than the majority of Martian meteorites that have been recovered so far, received considerable attention when a group of NASA scientists led by <a href="/wiki/David_S._McKay" title="David S. McKay">David S. McKay</a> reported microscopic features and geochemical anomalies that they considered to be best explained by the rock having hosted Martian bacteria in the distant past. Some of these features resembled terrestrial bacteria, aside from their being much smaller than any known form of life. Much controversy arose over this claim, and ultimately all of the evidence McKay's team cited as evidence of life was found to be explainable by non-biological processes. Although the scientific community has largely rejected the claim ALH 84001 contains evidence of ancient Martian life, the controversy associated with it is now seen as a historically significant moment in the development of exobiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disbelief_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-disbelief-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nakhla_meteorite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nakhla_meteorite.jpg/220px-Nakhla_meteorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nakhla_meteorite.jpg/330px-Nakhla_meteorite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nakhla_meteorite.jpg/440px-Nakhla_meteorite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nakhla_meteorite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nakhla meteorite</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nakhla">Nakhla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nakhla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nakhla_meteorite" title="Nakhla meteorite">Nakhla meteorite</a> fell on Earth on June 28, 1911, on the locality of Nakhla, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, a team from NASA's Johnson Space Center obtained a small sample for analysis. Researchers found preterrestrial aqueous alteration phases and objects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> of the size and shape consistent with Earthly <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossilized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanobacteria" title="Nanobacteria">nanobacteria</a>.
Analysis with <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography" title="Gas chromatography">gas chromatography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> (GC-MS) studied its high molecular weight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> in 2000, and NASA scientists concluded that as much as 75% of the organic compounds in Nakhla "may not be recent terrestrial contamination".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteoritos-Bio_192-3"><a href="#cite_note-meteoritos-Bio-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>This caused additional interest in this meteorite, so in 2006, NASA managed to obtain an additional and larger sample from the London Natural History Museum. On this second sample, a large dendritic <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> content was observed. When the results and evidence were published in 2006, some independent researchers claimed that the carbon deposits are of biologic origin. It was remarked that since carbon is the fourth most abundant element in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, finding it in curious patterns is not indicative or suggestive of biological origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shergotty">Shergotty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Shergotty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shergotty_meteorite" title="Shergotty meteorite">Shergotty meteorite</a>, a 4 kg Martian meteorite, fell on Earth on <a href="/wiki/Sherghati" title="Sherghati">Shergotty</a>, India on August 25, 1865, and was retrieved by witnesses almost immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_shergotty_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_shergotty-201">[201]</a></sup> It is composed mostly of <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> and thought to have undergone preterrestrial aqueous alteration for several centuries. Certain features in its interior suggest remnants of a biofilm and its associated microbial communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteoritos-Bio_192-4"><a href="#cite_note-meteoritos-Bio-192">[192]</a></sup> Work is in progress on searching for <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetites</a> within alteration phases.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yamato_000593">Yamato 000593</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Yamato 000593">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Yamato_000593" title="Yamato 000593">Yamato 000593</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Martian_meteorites#List" title="List of Martian meteorites">second largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> found on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Studies suggest the <a href="/wiki/Martian_meteorite" title="Martian meteorite">Martian meteorite</a> was formed about 1.3 billion years ago from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_flow" title="Lava flow">lava flow</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Impact_(mechanics)" title="Impact (mechanics)">impact</a> occurred on Mars about 12 million years ago and ejected the meteorite from the Martian surface into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>. The meteorite landed on Earth in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> about 50,000 years ago. The <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the meteorite is 13.7 kg (30 lb) and it has been found to contain evidence of past <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140227_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140227-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20140219_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20140219-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20140228_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20140228-204">[204]</a></sup> At a microscopic level, <a href="/wiki/Martian_spherules" title="Martian spherules">spheres</a> are found in the meteorite that are rich in <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> compared to surrounding areas that lack such spheres. The carbon-rich spheres may have been formed by <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic activity</a> according to NASA scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140227_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140227-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20140219_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20140219-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20140228_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20140228-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geysers_on_Mars">Geysers on Mars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Geysers on Mars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geysers_on_Mars" title="Geysers on Mars">Geysers on Mars</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:270px;max-width:270px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geysers_on_Mars.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="726" data-file-width="516" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Geysers_on_Mars.jpg/142px-Geysers_on_Mars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Geysers_on_Mars.jpg/213px-Geysers_on_Mars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Geysers_on_Mars.jpg/284px-Geysers_on_Mars.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Artist concept showing sand-laden jets erupt from geysers on Mars.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="321" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg/120px-Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg/180px-Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg/240px-Mars_Global_Surveyor_1.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Close up of dark dune spots, probably created by cold geyser-like eruptions.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>The seasonal frosting and defrosting of the southern ice cap results in the formation of spider-like radial channels carved on 1-meter thick ice by sunlight. Then, sublimed CO<sub>2</sub> – and probably water – increase pressure in their interior producing geyser-like eruptions of cold fluids often mixed with dark basaltic sand or mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006-100_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-2006-100-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kieffer2000_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kieffer2000-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Portyankina_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Portyankina-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugh2006_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh2006-208">[208]</a></sup> This process is rapid, observed happening in the space of a few days, weeks or months, a growth rate rather unusual in geology – especially for Mars.
</p><p>A team of Hungarian scientists propose that the geysers' most visible features, dark dune spots and spider channels, may be colonies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> Martian microorganisms, which over-winter beneath the ice cap, and as the <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> returns to the pole during early spring, light penetrates the ice, the microorganisms photosynthesize and heat their immediate surroundings. A pocket of liquid water, which would normally evaporate instantly in the thin Martian atmosphere, is trapped around them by the overlying ice. As this ice layer thins, the microorganisms show through grey. When the layer has completely melted, the microorganisms rapidly desiccate and turn black, surrounded by a grey aureole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andras_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andras-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pócs_et_al._2004_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pócs_et_al._2004-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> The Hungarian scientists believe that even a complex sublimation process is insufficient to explain the formation and evolution of the dark dune spots in space and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Since their discovery, fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a> promoted these formations as deserving of study from an <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiological</a> perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orme_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orme-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>A multinational European team suggests that if liquid water is present in the spiders' channels during their annual defrost cycle, they might provide a niche where certain microscopic life forms could have retreated and adapted while sheltered from solar radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manrubia_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manrubia-215">[215]</a></sup> A British team also considers the possibility that <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a>, or even simple plants might co-exist with these inorganic formations, especially if the mechanism includes liquid water and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal" title="Geothermal">geothermal</a> energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ness-216">[216]</a></sup> They also remark that the majority of geological structures may be accounted for without invoking any organic "life on Mars" hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ness-216">[216]</a></sup> It has been proposed to develop the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Geyser_Hopper" title="Mars Geyser Hopper">Mars Geyser Hopper</a> lander to study the geysers up close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiaa_50_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-aiaa_50-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forward_contamination">Forward contamination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Forward contamination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Planetary_protection" title="Planetary protection">Planetary protection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_contamination" title="Interplanetary contamination">Interplanetary contamination</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Planetary_protection" title="Planetary protection">Planetary protection</a> of Mars aims to prevent biological contamination of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strategy_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-strategy-218">[218]</a></sup> A major goal is to preserve the planetary record of natural processes by preventing human-caused microbial introductions, also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward_contamination" title="Forward contamination">forward contamination</a>.  There is abundant evidence as to what can happen when organisms from regions on Earth that have been isolated from one another for significant periods of time are introduced into each other's environment. Species that are constrained in one environment can thrive – often out of control – in another environment much to the detriment of the original species that were present.  In some ways, this problem could be compounded if life forms from one planet were introduced into the totally alien ecology of another world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowing_201304_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowing_201304-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>The prime concern of hardware contaminating Mars derives from incomplete spacecraft sterilization of some hardy terrestrial bacteria (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremophiles" title="Extremophiles">extremophiles</a>) despite best efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaty_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beaty-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debus_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debus-220">[220]</a></sup> Hardware includes landers, crashed probes, end-of-mission disposal of hardware, and the hard landing of entry, descent, and landing systems. This has prompted research on survival rates of <a href="/wiki/Radioresistance" title="Radioresistance">radiation-resistant microorganisms</a> including the species <i><a href="/wiki/Deinococcus_radiodurans" title="Deinococcus radiodurans">Deinococcus radiodurans</a></i> and genera <i><a href="/wiki/Brevundimonas" title="Brevundimonas">Brevundimonas</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodococcus" title="Rhodococcus">Rhodococcus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas" title="Pseudomonas">Pseudomonas</a></i> under simulated Martian conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_protection_–_radiodurans_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary_protection_–_radiodurans-221">[221]</a></sup> Results from one of these experimental irradiation experiments, combined with previous radiation modeling, indicate that <i><a href="/wiki/Brevundimonas" title="Brevundimonas">Brevundimonas</a></i> sp. MV.7 emplaced only 30 cm deep in Martian dust could survive the cosmic radiation for up to 100,000 years before suffering 10⁶ population reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_protection_–_radiodurans_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary_protection_–_radiodurans-221">[221]</a></sup> The diurnal Mars-like cycles in temperature and relative humidity affected the viability of <i>Deinococcus radiodurans</i> cells quite severely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simulation_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-simulation-222">[222]</a></sup> In other simulations, <i>Deinococcus radiodurans</i> also failed to grow under low atmospheric pressure, under 0 °C, or in the absence of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serratia_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-serratia-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Survival_under_simulated_Martian_conditions">Survival under simulated Martian conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Survival under simulated Martian conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 26 April 2012, scientists reported that an <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a> survived and showed remarkable results on the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_capacity" title="Adaptive capacity">adaptation capacity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic activity</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Simulation" title="Simulation">simulation</a> time of 34 days under Martian conditions in the Mars Simulation Laboratory (MSL) maintained by the <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">German Aerospace Center</a> (DLR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lab_study_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-lab_study-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EGU-20120426_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-EGU-20120426-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dlrMarsStudy_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-dlrMarsStudy-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fungal_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-fungal-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nordita_eana2012_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-nordita_eana2012-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lichen_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-lichen-229">[229]</a></sup> The ability to survive in an environment is not the same as the ability to thrive, reproduce, and evolve in that same environment, necessitating further study.
</p><p>Although numerous studies point to resistance to some of Mars conditions, they do so separately, and none has considered the full range of Martian surface conditions, including temperature, pressure, atmospheric composition, radiation, humidity, oxidizing regolith, and others, all at the same time and in combination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Laboratory simulations show that whenever multiple lethal factors are combined, the survival rates plummet quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust-up_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-dust-up-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions">Missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars-2">Mars-2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mars-2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mars_program" title="Mars program">Mars program</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mars_program" title="Mars program">Mars-1</a> was the first spacecraft launched to Mars in 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> but communication was lost while en route to Mars. With <a href="/wiki/Mars_2" title="Mars 2">Mars-2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_3" title="Mars 3">Mars-3</a> in 1971-1972, information was obtained on the nature of the surface rocks and altitude profiles of the surface density of the soil, its thermal conductivity, and thermal anomalies detected on the surface of Mars. The program found that its northern polar cap has a temperature below -110 °C and that the water vapor content in the atmosphere of Mars is five thousand times less than on Earth. No signs of life were found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mariner_4">Mariner 4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Mariner 4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:428px;max-width:428px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_m04_11e.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2433" data-file-width="2529" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mars_m04_11e.jpg/220px-Mars_m04_11e.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mars_m04_11e.jpg/330px-Mars_m04_11e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mars_m04_11e.jpg/440px-Mars_m04_11e.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Mariner Crater, as seen by Mariner 4 in 1965. Pictures like this suggested that Mars is too dry for any kind of life.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="410" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg/200px-Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg/300px-Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg/400px-Streamlined_Islands_in_Maja_Valles.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Streamlined Islands seen by Viking orbiter showed that large floods occurred on Mars. The image is located in <a href="/wiki/Lunae_Palus_quadrangle" title="Lunae Palus quadrangle">Lunae Palus quadrangle</a>.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mariner_4" title="Mariner 4">Mariner 4</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mariner_4" title="Mariner 4">Mariner 4</a> probe performed the first successful <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> of the planet Mars, returning the first pictures of the Martian surface in 1965. The photographs showed an arid Mars without rivers, oceans, or any signs of life. Further, it revealed that the surface (at least the parts that it photographed) was covered in craters, indicating a lack of plate tectonics and weathering of any kind for the last 4 billion years. The probe also found that Mars has no <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">global magnetic field</a> that would protect the planet from potentially life-threatening <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a>. The probe was able to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> on the planet to be about 0.6 kPa (compared to Earth's 101.3 kPa), meaning that liquid water could not exist on the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chambers_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-17">[17]</a></sup> After Mariner 4, the search for life on Mars changed to a search for bacteria-like living organisms rather than for multicellular organisms, as the environment was clearly too harsh for these.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viking_orbiters">Viking orbiters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Viking orbiters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking program</a></div>
<p>Liquid water is necessary for known life and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, so if water was present on Mars, the chances of it having supported life may have been determinant. The Viking orbiters found evidence of possible river valleys in many areas, erosion and, in the southern hemisphere, branched streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cratering_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-cratering-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raeburn_1998_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-raeburn_1998-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moore_atlas_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-moore_atlas-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viking_experiments">Viking experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Viking experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_biological_experiments" title="Viking biological experiments">Viking biological experiments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sagan_Viking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2468" data-file-width="3678" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Sagan_Viking.jpg/220px-Sagan_Viking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Sagan_Viking.jpg/330px-Sagan_Viking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Sagan_Viking.jpg/440px-Sagan_Viking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sagan_Viking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> poses next to a replica of the Viking landers.</div></div></div>
<p>The primary mission of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_probes" title="Viking probes">Viking probes</a> of the mid-1970s was to carry out experiments designed to detect microorganisms in Martian soil because the favorable conditions for the evolution of multicellular organisms ceased some four billion years ago on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BC_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-BC-236">[236]</a></sup> The tests were formulated to look for microbial life similar to that found on Earth. Of the four experiments, only the Labeled Release (LR) experiment returned a positive result,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2013)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Life_on_Mars#Dubious" title="Talk:Life on Mars">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> showing increased <sup>14</sup>CO<sub>2</sub> production on first exposure of soil to water and nutrients. All scientists agree on two points from the Viking missions: that radiolabeled <sup>14</sup>CO<sub>2</sub> was evolved in the Labeled Release experiment, and that the <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography%E2%80%93mass_spectrometry" title="Gas chromatography–mass spectrometry">GCMS</a> detected no organic molecules. There are vastly different interpretations of what those results imply.
</p><p>A 2011 <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> textbook notes that the GCMS was the decisive factor due to which "For most of the Viking scientists, the final conclusion was that the <i>Viking</i> missions failed to detect life in the Martian soil."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlaxcoGross2011_1_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlaxcoGross2011_1-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the designers of the Labeled Release experiment, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Levin" title="Gilbert Levin">Gilbert Levin</a>, believes his results are a definitive diagnostic for life on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chambers_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-17">[17]</a></sup> Levin's interpretation is disputed by many scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-238">[238]</a></sup> A 2006 <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> textbook noted that "With unsterilized Terrestrial samples, though, the addition of more nutrients after the initial incubation would then produce still more radioactive gas as the dormant bacteria sprang into action to consume the new dose of food. This was not true of the Martian soil; on Mars, the second and third nutrient injections did not produce any further release of labeled gas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plaxco2006_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plaxco2006-239">[239]</a></sup> Other scientists argue that <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxides</a> in the soil could have produced this effect without life being present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlaxcoGross2011_2_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlaxcoGross2011_2-240">[240]</a></sup> An almost general consensus discarded the Labeled Release data as evidence of life, because the gas chromatograph and mass spectrometer, designed to identify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_organic_matter" title="Natural organic matter">natural organic matter</a>, did not detect organic molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-177">[177]</a></sup> More recently, high levels of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic chemicals</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chlorobenzene" title="Chlorobenzene">chlorobenzene</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mars_Science_Laboratory#Detection_of_organics" title="Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory">detected</a> in powder drilled from one of the rocks, named "<a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars" title="List of rocks on Mars">Cumberland</a>", analyzed by the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-153">[153]</a></sup> The results of the Viking mission concerning life are considered by the general expert community as inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chambers_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlaxcoGross2011_2_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlaxcoGross2011_2-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci_99_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci_99-241">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, during a Seminar of the Geophysical Laboratory of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institution" title="Carnegie Institution">Carnegie Institution</a> (Washington, D.C., US), <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Levin" title="Gilbert Levin">Gilbert Levin</a>'s investigation was assessed once more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_177-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-177">[177]</a></sup> Levin still maintains that his original data were correct, as the positive and negative control experiments were in order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bianciardi-2012_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bianciardi-2012-180">[180]</a></sup>  Moreover, Levin's team, on 12 April 2012, reported a statistical speculation, based on old data —reinterpreted mathematically through <a href="/wiki/Cluster_analysis" title="Cluster analysis">cluster analysis</a>— of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_biological_experiments#Labeled_Release" title="Viking biological experiments">Labeled Release experiments</a>, that may suggest evidence of "extant microbial life on Mars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bianciardi-2012_180-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bianciardi-2012-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_201204_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_201204-242">[242]</a></sup> Critics counter that the method has not yet been proven effective for differentiating between biological and non-biological processes on Earth so it is premature to draw any conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery-20120412_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery-20120412-243">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>A research team from the <a href="/wiki/National_Autonomous_University_of_Mexico" title="National Autonomous University of Mexico">National Autonomous University of Mexico</a> headed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rafael_Navarro-Gonz%C3%A1lez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rafael Navarro-González (page does not exist)">Rafael Navarro-González</a> concluded that the GCMS equipment (TV-GC-MS) used by the <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking program</a> to search for organic molecules, may not be sensitive enough to detect low levels of organics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro-184">[184]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Biemann" title="Klaus Biemann">Klaus Biemann</a>, the principal investigator of the GCMS experiment on <i>Viking</i> wrote a rebuttal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nas_104_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-nas_104-244">[244]</a></sup> Because of the simplicity of sample handling, TV–GC–MS is still considered the standard method for organic detection on future Mars missions, so Navarro-González suggests that the design of future organic instruments for Mars should include other methods of detection.
</p><p>After the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorates</a> on Mars by the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)">Phoenix lander</a>, practically the same team of Navarro-González published a paper arguing that the Viking GCMS results were compromised by the presence of perchlorates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-245">[245]</a></sup> A 2011 astrobiology textbook notes that "while perchlorate is too poor an oxidizer to reproduce the LR results (under the conditions of that experiment perchlorate does not oxidize organics), it does oxidize, and thus destroy, organics at the higher temperatures used in the Viking GCMS experiment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlaxcoGross2011_1_2nd_ed_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlaxcoGross2011_1_2nd_ed-246">[246]</a></sup> Biemann has written a commentary critical of this Navarro-González paper as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_3869_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi_3869-247">[247]</a></sup> to which the latter have replied;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_3880_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi_3880-248">[248]</a></sup> the exchange was published in December 2011.
</p>
<h3><span id="Phoenix_lander.2C_2008"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Phoenix_lander,_2008">Phoenix lander, 2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Phoenix lander, 2008">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)">Phoenix (spacecraft)</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pia09344.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5000" data-file-width="7984" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Pia09344.jpg/220px-Pia09344.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Pia09344.jpg/330px-Pia09344.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Pia09344.jpg/440px-Pia09344.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pia09344.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An artist's concept of the Phoenix spacecraft</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)">Phoenix</a> mission landed a robotic spacecraft in the polar region of Mars on May 25, 2008 and it operated until November 10, 2008. One of the mission's two primary objectives was to search for a "habitable zone" in the Martian <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a> where microbial life could exist, the other main goal being to study the <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geological</a> history of water on Mars. The lander has a 2.5 meter robotic arm that was capable of digging shallow trenches in the regolith. There was an electrochemistry experiment which analysed the <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> in the regolith and the amount and type of <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a> on Mars. The <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking program</a> data indicate that oxidants on Mars may vary with latitude, noting that <a href="/wiki/Viking_2" title="Viking 2">Viking 2</a> saw fewer oxidants than <a href="/wiki/Viking_1" title="Viking 1">Viking 1</a> in its more northerly position. Phoenix landed further north still.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarsDaily_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarsDaily-249">[249]</a></sup>
Phoenix's preliminary data revealed that Mars soil contains <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorate</a>, and thus may not be as life-friendly as thought earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perchlorate_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-perchlorate-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-la_4986721_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-la_4986721-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD_2008-08_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-SD_2008-08-252">[252]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and salinity level were viewed as benign from the standpoint of biology. The analysers also indicated the presence of bound water and CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phoenix_sol_30_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-phoenix_sol_30-253">[253]</a></sup>
A recent analysis of Martian meteorite EETA79001 found 0.6 ppm ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, 1.4 ppm ClO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, and 16 ppm NO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, most likely of Martian origin.  The ClO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> suggests presence of other highly oxidizing oxychlorines such as ClO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> or ClO, produced both by UV oxidation of Cl and X-ray radiolysis of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. Thus only highly refractory and/or well-protected (sub-surface) organics are likely to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup>
In addition, recent analysis of the Phoenix WCL showed that the Ca(ClO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> in the Phoenix soil has not interacted with liquid water of any form, perhaps for as long as 600 Myr. If it had, the highly soluble Ca(ClO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> in contact with liquid water would have formed only CaSO<sub>4</sub>. This suggests a severely arid environment, with minimal or no liquid water interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7295" data-file-width="5247" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg/170px-PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg/255px-PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg/340px-PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA16239_High-Resolution_Self-Portrait_by_Curiosity_Rover_Arm_Camera.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> self-portrait.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_Science_Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Mars Science Laboratory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curiosity_rover" title="Curiosity rover">Curiosity rover</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory">Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory</a></div>
<p>The  <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a> mission is a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> project that launched on November 26, 2011,  the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>, a nuclear-powered robotic vehicle, bearing instruments designed to assess past and present <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability</a> conditions on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_msl_launch_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_msl_launch-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-MSL_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-MSL-257">[257]</a></sup> The <i>Curiosity</i> rover landed on Mars on <a href="/wiki/Aeolis_Palus" title="Aeolis Palus">Aeolis Palus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale Crater</a>, near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolis_Mons" title="Aeolis Mons">Aeolis Mons</a> (a.k.a. Mount Sharp),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-20120516_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-20120516-258">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120327_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120327-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120328_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120328-260">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120329_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120329-261">[261]</a></sup> on August 6, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale_Crater_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale_Crater-262">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale_Crater2_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale_Crater2-263">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale_Crater3_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale_Crater3-264">[264]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 16 December 2014, NASA reported the <i>Curiosity</i> rover detected a "tenfold spike", likely localized, in the amount of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmosphere</a>. Sample measurements taken "a dozen times over 20 months" showed increases in late 2013 and early 2014, averaging "7 parts of methane per billion in the atmosphere." Before and after that, readings averaged around one-tenth that level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_152-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-153">[153]</a></sup>
In addition, low levels of <a href="/wiki/Chlorobenzene" title="Chlorobenzene">chlorobenzene</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>Cl</span>), were detected in powder drilled from one of the rocks, named "<a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars" title="List of rocks on Mars">Cumberland</a>", analyzed by the Curiosity rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_152-4"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_153-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_astrobiology_missions">Future astrobiology missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Future astrobiology missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/ExoMars" title="ExoMars">ExoMars</a> is a European-led multi-spacecraft programme currently under development by the European Space Agency (ESA) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federal_Space_Agency" title="Russian Federal Space Agency">Russian Federal Space Agency</a> for launch in 2016 and 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_signed_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_signed-266">[266]</a></sup> Its primary scientific mission will be to search for possible <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> on Mars, past or present. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExoMars_rover" title="ExoMars rover">rover</a> with a 2 m (6.6 ft) core drill will be used to sample various depths beneath the surface where liquid water may be found and where microorganisms (or organic biosignatures<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) might survive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wall-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> – The <i>Mars 2020</i> rover is a  <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> <a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">planetary rover mission</a> by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> with a planned launch in 2020. It is intended to investigate an <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiologically</a> relevant ancient environment on Mars, investigate its surface <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">geological processes</a> and history, including the assessment of its past <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability</a> and potential for preservation of <a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> within accessible geological materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowing_2020_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowing_2020-267">[267]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Sample_Return_Mission" title="Mars Sample Return Mission">Mars Sample Return Mission</a> — The best life detection experiment proposed is the examination on Earth of a soil sample from Mars. However, the difficulty of providing and maintaining life support over the months of transit from Mars to Earth remains to be solved. Providing for still unknown environmental and nutritional requirements is daunting. Should dead organisms be found in a sample, it would be difficult to conclude that those organisms were alive when obtained.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_colonization_of_Mars">Human colonization of Mars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Life_on_Mars&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Human colonization of Mars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars" title="Colonization of Mars">Colonization of Mars</a></div>
<p>Some of the main reasons for colonizing Mars include economic interests, long-term scientific research best carried out by humans as opposed to robotic probes, and sheer curiosity. Surface conditions and the presence of <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water on Mars</a> make it arguably the most <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">hospitable of the planets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, other than <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Human colonization of Mars would require <i>in situ</i> resource utilization (<a href="/wiki/In_situ_resource_utilization" title="In situ resource utilization">ISRU</a>); A NASA report states that "applicable frontier technologies include robotics, machine intelligence, nanotechnology, synthetic biology, <a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">3-D printing/additive</a> manufacturing, and autonomy. These technologies combined with the vast natural resources should enable, pre- and post-human arrival ISRU to greatly increase reliability and safety and reduce cost for human colonization of Mars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceref_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-spaceref-269">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-race_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-race-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-cosmic_radiation-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-cosmic_radiation_47-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-cosmic_radiation_47-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Than, Ker (January 29, 2007). <a class="external text" href="http://www.space.com/3396-study-surface-mars-devoid-life.html" rel="nofollow">"Study: Surface of Mars Devoid of Life"</a>. <i>Space.com</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140429215220/http://www.space.com/3396-study-surface-mars-devoid-life.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on April 29, 2014. <q>After mapping cosmic radiation levels at various depths on Mars, researchers have concluded that any life within the first several yards of the planet's surface would be killed by lethal doses of cosmic radiation.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Space.com&amp;rft.atitle=Study%3A+Surface+of+Mars+Devoid+of+Life&amp;rft.date=2007-01-29&amp;rft.aulast=Than&amp;rft.aufirst=Ker&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.space.com%2F3396-study-surface-mars-devoid-life.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALife+on+Mars"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Dartnell-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dartnell_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dartnell_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Dartnell, L. R.; Desorgher, L.; Ward, J. M.; Coates, A. J. (2007). "Modelling the surface and subsurface Martian radiation environment: Implications for astrobiology". <i>Geophysical Research Letters</i>. <b>34</b> (2): L02207. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2007GeoRL..34.2207D" rel="nofollow">2007GeoRL..34.2207D</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1029%2F2006GL027494" rel="nofollow">10.1029/2006GL027494</a>. <q>Bacteria or spores held dormant by freezing conditions cannot metabolise and become inactivated by accumulating radiation damage. We find that at 2 m depth, the reach of the ExoMars drill, a population of radioresistant cells would need to have reanimated within the last 450,000 years to still be viable. Recovery of viable cells cryopreserved within the putative Cerberus pack-ice requires a drill depth of at least 7.5 m.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Geophysical+Research+Letters&amp;rft.atitle=Modelling+the+surface+and+subsurface+Martian+radiation+environment%3A+Implications+for+astrobiology&amp;rft.volume=34&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=L02207&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1029%2F2006GL027494&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2007GeoRL..34.2207D&amp;rft.aulast=Dartnell&amp;rft.aufirst=L.+R.&amp;rft.au=Desorgher%2C+L.&amp;rft.au=Ward%2C+J.+M.&amp;rft.au=Coates%2C+A.+J.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALife+on+Mars"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
